To take up the point of the noble Earl, Lord Erroll, on financial services, in July last year the Financial Services Authority (FSA) made it absolutely clear that, in its view, the Bill has utility. It said that in the long term, if identity cards were a more effective token of identity than anything currently available, they could contribute substantially to the fight against crime and reduce the cost to industry. Those matters are clear.

It is also true that the widespread use of false documents is an essential aspect of terrorist activities. Al'Qaeda's training manual requires its operatives to acquire false identities to hide their terrorist activities. We believe, therefore, that ID cards will make it harder for them to do that.

I am conscious of the time. The noble Lord, Lord Dholakia, raised the issue of border controls and how they will be strengthened. We believe that ID cards will help to strengthen our borders against organised criminals for many of the reasons to which I have just alluded.

The noble Lord also mentioned devolution and the difference that that will bring. As with passports or immigration documents the issue of identity cards will apply uniformly UK-wide. Therefore, the Bill covers matters that are reserved to the UK Parliament. However, the use of identity cards to access public services that are devolved will be matters for the devolved administrations. Some would say that the joy and the whole point of devolution is that those differences will be possible.

I was asked about costs. The costs are £186 million for set up over the first three years; £415 million is the annual cost for biometric passports by 2008-09; £85 million is the annual cost for ID cards; £50 million is the annual cost for verification; and £85 is the estimated cost of an ID card/passport package. Those remain our current and best estimates. The noble Baroness, Lady Falkner of Margravine, will therefore forgive me, perhaps, for having risen to my feet at her £5 billion, because that is significantly different from our figures.

Both the noble Baroness, Lady Anelay of St Johns, and the noble Baroness, Lady Seccombe, raised their five points. First, legislation must clearly define the purpose of the cards. The Identity Cards Bill sets out statutory purposes at Clause 1, and makes clear that identity cards are provided to individuals as security and a reliable method of proving identity; for helping to identify people securely in the public interest; to help prevent and detect crime; to help enforce controls on immigration and illegal working; to safeguard national security; and to deliver more efficient and effective public services.

Secondly, is the technology sufficiently well developed and robust? We say that it is. The Government have always made clear that, before the final move to compulsion, there will be a rigorous evaluation to ensure that the technology is working and that public services have implemented the technology and business changes necessary to take full advantage of the scheme.

Is the Home Office capable of making the cards work? The short answer is yes. The identity cards project is subject to regular scrutiny by the Office of Government Commerce, a gateway review process. The ID cards will be issued by a new agency, building on the existing UK Passport Agency. The noble Baroness, Lady Anelay of St Johns, will remember the

Clause 42 [Regulation of public charitable collections]:

The Deputy Chairman of Committees (Lord Brougham and Vaux): Welcome to the eighth and, we hope, last day of the Charities Bill. There will be a Division in the Chamber, so whoever is speaking when it occurs will have to stop. We shall then return and play it by ear. If there is another Division, we will go off again.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts moved Amendment No. 183AA:

    Page 45, line 30, leave out subsection (4).

The noble Lord said: Once more into the breach, dear friends. We now come to Part 3, which concerns collections and funding. The amendment concerns Clause 42. The Explanatory Notes state:

    "This clause and the following 21 clauses . . . provide for the regulation of public charitable, philanthropic and benevolent collections. They build on provisions in Part 3 of the 1992 Act . . . [which] was never brought into effect, and will be replaced by these provisions".

We have already commented on the fact that Part 3 of the 1992 Act was insufficiently scrutinised before being passed in the wash-up period before the 1992 general election. Let us hope that we do not make the same mistake again.

We have tabled this amendment because it seems rather odd to have a further definition of "public charitable collection" given in subsection (4) when other definitions exist earlier in the clause. Subsection (1) gives the two definitions of public charitable collections, being,

    "collections in a public place; and . . . door to door collections",

in paragraphs (a) and (b), with subsection (2)(a) defining when it is a public charitable collection as opposed to a charitable appeal and subsection (2)(b) defining charitable appeal.

Considering that those definitions are given, it seems confusing then to provide in subsection (4) a further definition of when an appeal to members of the public is a public charitable collection. Could not the definition of an appeal as given in subsection (4)(a) be transferred to the definition of charitable appeal in subsection (2)(b)? Subsection (4)(b) seems to be
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC2
 
superfluous, as it simply refers to subsection (2) and mirrors its wording. Surely the drafting could be more economic or succinct, so that there is not such confusion about the exact whereabouts in the Bill of the precise definition of public charitable collection.

We have said many times that simplicity and clarity should be our watchwords in providing the regulatory framework for the charitable sector. I beg to move.

Lord Phillips of Sudbury: I applaud the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, for moving an amendment that may—we have yet to hear—achieve clarity, and so on. I find the drafting of Clauses 42 to 44 very difficult, and I say that as an old and battered lawyer. I hate to think what members of the public—I am thinking of the charities—may make of that when trying to find their way round this extremely convoluted wording and definition. I note that public charitable collection is the same as charitable appeal, which is defined as,

    "an appeal for charitable, benevolent or philanthropic purposes".

There is no definition anywhere in the Bill of what are philanthropic or benevolent purposes. If there is no unforeseen objection to the amendment to the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, I am right behind him.

Lord Bassam of Brighton: As the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, said, this is an important part of the Bill dealing with the regulation of public charitable collections. It will replace the current legislation, which is outdated, fragmented and has been somewhat inconsistently applied. The current arrangements in place for street collections derive from a law of 1916, and the arrangements for house-to-house collections from a law of 1939. The Bill creates a new unified licensing scheme for public collections that removes the inconsistencies and ensures what we believe to be proportionate regulation. I look forward to explaining the Government's policy as we debate the various amendments.

Clause 42 defines the two types of public charitable collections: collections in a public place—often referred to as street collections—and door-to-door collections. It also provides a number of definitions for the purposes of this chapter, including that a charitable appeal includes giving to philanthropic and benevolent purposes; the definition of what constitutes a public place; and that giving includes giving by whatever means, which would include by direct debit—thus ensuring that face-to-face fundraising is properly regulated.

Under existing legislation, commercial participators who sell products house-to-house or in a public place, where a proportion of the profits go to a charity, would in most cases require a licence from the relevant local authority in whose area they were operating, as the activity would be considered to be a charitable appeal.

The Bill does not change that. Under subsection (4), the sale of goods in a public place where part of the proceeds were to be applied to charitable, philanthropic or benevolent purposes would be considered to be a
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC3
 
charitable appeal, and therefore the licensing provisions of the Bill would apply. We believe that that is the right approach.

The Bill is deregulatory for door-to-door collections. Organisations proposing to undertake door-to-door collections would require a public collections certificate from the Charity Commission to demonstrate their eligibility to collect, which could be in force for a period of up to five years. They would be required only to notify local authorities for the areas in which they proposed to collect and the details of their proposed collections, rather than apply for individual licences for each collection from local authorities, as is currently the case. Some commercial participators would be able to operate under a public collections certificate that had been granted to the charity for which they were raising funds in the public collection, rather than requiring their own public collections certificate.

The amendment would remove the requirement on commercial participators undertaking charitable appeals in a public place, or door-to-door, to be bound by the licensing scheme for public charitable collections. We do not believe that that is desirable. Local authorities want to know what legitimate collecting activity is taking place within their areas, either door-to-door or in public places. By being aware of all the legitimate collections that are taking place, local authorities will be able quickly to identify any unlicensed activity that is occurring and take action. If this amendment were to be accepted, it would create a loophole that could be exploited as a means of avoiding the licensing requirements.

In response to the consultation on those proposals the point was made by a number of respondents, including local authorities and charities, that commercial participators should be properly covered by the licensing scheme to ensure that local authorities know what legitimate collection activities are being carried out. We do not therefore intend to amend the Bill further to deregulate door-to-door collections or collections in a public place for commercial participators, although in preparing regulations and guidance we will be happy to consider what scope there would be for a different approach with regard to the conduct of those collections—for example, in relation to the wearing of badges or the keeping of accounts. We also propose to produce a plain English guide to the Bill, which would be for charities, members of the public and hard-pressed lawyers.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: I am grateful to the Minister; I think that he read out his speaking note beautifully. The only trouble was that it did not quite answer my question, which was: could we not redraft the Bill so that subsection (4) became subsection (2), to bring it all into one place?

The point has been made to us that the way in which the definitions expand through the clauses is unhelpful. People working in the charitable sector should be able to go to one place to get the definition for which they are looking.
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC4
 

I understand what the Minister said. I am grateful to the noble Lord, Lord Phillips, for his support. I share the view that this is still not very clear. I am sorry that we have not been able to persuade the Government to clarify the matter instead of having further definitions running down the page.

Lord Bassam of Brighton: In an effort to answer the noble Lord's question, his point about drafting issues is fair. We can have a discussion with parliamentary counsel about that, but I will not promise that we will make a big and fundamental change at this stage, which would be rather difficult.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: That is a handsome offer for which I am grateful. I therefore beg leave to withdraw the amendment.

Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

Clause 43 [Charitable appeals that are not public charitable collections]:

Lord Bassam of Brighton moved Amendment No. 183AB:

    Page 46, line 28, after "only" insert—


"(i) "

The noble Lord said: In moving Amendment No. 183AB in the name of my noble friend Lady Scotland, I shall speak also to the other amendments in this group.

We are grateful in this instance to the National Trust for pointing out the need for these amendments. Under Clause 43 as drafted, a charitable appeal which takes place on land where members of the public have access only by permission of the occupier, and where the occupier is the promoter of the appeal, does not count as a public charitable collection. It is therefore not regulated under the Bill. The purpose of that provision is to allow charities to organise their own collections on their own property without having to obtain a public collections certificate or a local authority permit. So in a sense, this is a deregulatory move, but a very sensible one.

The National Trust reminded us that it and some other landowning charities owned land to which the public now has a statutory right of access—for example, under the Countryside and Rights of Way Act 2000. Members of the public who exercise their statutory right of access are of course not on the land by permission of the occupier so, as the Bill is drafted, Clause 43 would not apply. That would mean that any appeal organised by a charity on its own land to which the public have a statutory right of access would be fully regulated. We do not think that is a justifiable burden, so Amendments Nos. 183AB and 183AC will put appeals on statutory access land on to the same footing as appeals on land where the public go by permission of the occupying charity. I should add, for the avoidance of doubt, that if the charity itself is not the promoter of the appeal on its own land—and is not therefore in a position to oversee and control the appeal—the appeal will fall to be regulated like any other collection.
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC5
 

Amendments Nos. 183HC and 183HD similarly alter the grounds on which a local authority can refuse a permit. One of those grounds is when a collection is to take place on the same day, or on the day before or after, another collection, except that the local authority may not refuse a permit on those grounds if, first, the collection is going to take place in one location on land to which the public have access by the occupier's permission; and, secondly, the occupier has given its consent to the collection. The effect of Amendments Nos. 183HC and 183HD is to extend that to cover statutory access land as well as land where the public go with the occupier's permission. I beg to move.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: I am grateful for the Minister's explanation of this provision, which seems sensible. Clause 46 [Restrictions on conducting door to door collection]:

3.45 p.m.

Lord Bassam of Brighton moved Amendment No. 183ZZA:

    Page 48, line 6, leave out "before the date on which the collection commences" and insert "falling before the day (or the first of the days) on which the collection takes place—"

The noble Lord said: In moving the amendment, I shall also speak to Amendments Nos. 183CZB and 183GA, which stand in the name of my noble friend Lady Scotland. I hope that I will be able to provide the noble Lord with some reassurance about his amendment.
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC6
 

This group of amendments relates to the period within which a promoter must notify the local authority of a proposed door-to-door collection or apply for a public collections certificate or permit to collect in a public place. Amendment No. 183BZC deals with the same point in relation to the notification of a local short-term collection, but falls outside this grouping.

A promoter of door-to-door collections must, under Clause 46, hold a valid public collections certificate, issued by the Charity Commission and in force for up to five years. This demonstrates the bona fides of the promoter and enables the promoter to conduct door-to-door collections, provided that he notifies the relevant local authority of the collection or collections.

A promoter of a collection in a public place must also hold a valid public collections certificate and must apply to the relevant local authority for a permit to conduct the collection at the time and place proposed. The need for a permit to conduct this type of collection is to ensure that the area is not swamped with public collections exceeding its capacity.

A promoter of a short-term collection need not hold a valid public collections certificate, and need only notify the local authority of the proposed collection. Unless the local authority is satisfied that the proposed collection is not an exempt local short-term collection or that the promoter has been in breach of regulations or convicted of a relevant offence, and in either case serves notice on the promoter to that effect, the promoter can proceed with the proposed collection.

The Joint Committee recommended that we revisit the notification requirements as set out in the draft Bill, in the light of representations made by sector representatives questioning the workability of the notification requirements in the draft Bill. In response to the Joint Committee's recommendation, the Government intend that the notification requirements be prescribed in regulations made by the Secretary of State following further consultation. A further advantage of prescribing the notification requirement in regulations is that it provides flexibility to update them in the light of experience or future changes in the public collections field.

Amendment No. 183B, to be moved by the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, would specify in the Bill the period within which a promoter must notify the local authority of a proposed door-to-door collection. This would go against the policy that the notification requirements, including the period within which the notification must be made, should be prescribed in regulations after consultation.

I agree that the notification periods to be prescribed must allow charities to plan and co-ordinate their collections, but this is probably best achieved through regulations following consultation. On that basis, I hope that the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, will withdraw his amendment after he moves it.

Amendments Nos. 183ZZA, 183CZB and 183GA correct the drafting of the periods within which notification of a door-to-door collection must be given
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC7
 
or an application for a public collections certificate or permit to collect made. We realise that the existing formula within the prescribed period does not do what was intended. The intention is to require notice to be given within a period at least a certain amount of time before the proposed collection starts. But as the Bill is drafted, if the prescribed period were 14 days, then within 14 days would mean 14 days or less, giving the opposite result and making it unworkable for local authorities. The new formulation makes the intention clear.

Amendment No. 183CZB corrects the drafting in Clause 48 in respect of an application to the Charity Commission for a public collections certificate. Amendment No. 183GA corrects the drafting in respect of an application for a permit to collect in a public place. The next group of amendments deals with notification of local short-term collections. 4 p.m.

Amendment No. 183BZB replaces subsection (3)(b) of Clause 46, which relates to notifying a local authority of when a door-to-door collection is to take place. The new wording refers to the promoter specifying the prescribed particulars of when the proposed collection is to be conducted. The particulars to be prescribed by regulations made under Clause 60 could include the date on which a single collection is to take place, a series of dates for a series of collections, or a period within which several collections of unspecified dates would take place. In drawing up the regulations, we shall take heed of charities' concerns to be able properly to plan their collecting activity. I know, for example, that charity shops often have arrangements with local authorities whereby they are licensed to collect door-to-door over a period without needing to provide the dates of the individual collections. I made reference with regard to the previous group of amendments that we shall consult widely prior to drawing up the regulations to ensure that they will be workable.

Government Amendments Nos. 183BZC, 183BZD, 183BB, and 183CZA all relate to Clause 47, which makes provision for local, short-term collections to be exempt from all the licensing requirements. That is to ensure that locally organised, infrequent, small-scale collecting activity can take place without the need to obtain a public collections certificate or permit to collect. The requirement for that type of collection would simply be to notify the local authority of the proposed collection, although the promoter would still have to ensure that it complied with regulations about its conduct. I shall explain how regulations will prescribe what types of collections would be eligible for this exemption when we come on to debate amendments to Clause 60.

Amendment No. 183BZC restructures subsection (1) of Clause 47. The revised wording draws a distinction between "local, short-term collections" and "exempt collections". A local, short-term collection is one that is local in character, where the duration of the appeal does not exceed the prescribed period of time. The amendment provides for this in new subsection (1A). A collection is exempt if it satisfies the test for a local, short-term collection, is properly notified to the local authority, and the local authority does not serve a notice refusing to allow the collection for the reasons specified in subsection (3).

The revised wording in this amendment also serves two other purposes. It picks up the point mentioned earlier in debating the previous group of amendments,
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC11
 
by correcting the drafting in subsection (1)(b) to allow the period within which notice of the collection must be given to be properly prescribed. It has also been modified so that the prescribed period for local authorities to serve notice refusing to allow the collection begins by reference to the date on which the local authority is notified, instead of ending by reference to the date when the proposed collection commences. We believe that that improves the drafting of the clause.

Subsection (2) of Clause 47 provides the matters that must be notified to the local authority. Amendment No. 183BZD updates the cross-reference in subsection (2) in light of the restructuring of subsection (1) by Amendment No. 183BZC. Amendment No. 183BB replaces existing subsections (3) and (4). As currently drafted, it enables a local authority to serve notice refusing to allow a proposed local, short-term collection, on the grounds that it is not a local or short-term collection within the prescribed meaning. This amendment gives local authorities an additional ground to refuse to allow a proposed local, short-term collection. The new ground is that the promoter has been convicted of a relevant criminal offence, or breached regulations made under Clause 60. This new ground will avoid a local authority being unable to prevent such a collection going ahead, even though it had serious concerns about the conduct of the collection due to the promoter's track record. Amendment No. 183CZA replaces subsection (6)(b) to reflect the changes to the structure of this clause.

Amendments Nos. 183BA, 183MA and 183NA would enable regulations made under Clause 60 to allow the notification of a series of local short-term collections exempt from the licensing regime to take place on specified or unspecified dates within a period.

While we agree that that is a sensible approach for door-to-door collections, where some local authorities currently follow this practice with local charity shops, we do not think it appropriate for local, short-term collections, which could be in a public place or door-to-door collections. Our view is that local, short-term collections will be locally organised collections of a small scale, taking place within a short period of time, and not repeated with any frequency or forming part of a series of collections over time. The amendment would allow regulations to prescribe that a series of collections could take place on unspecified dates with a period notified to the local authority. We do not believe that that reflects the intended purpose of local, short-term collections.

Amendment No. 183NA would also make provision for regulations to allow a periodically repeated series of collections which could take place within a year and be notified up to one year in advance of the last collection in the series. The regulations could permit that in the case of door-to-door collections. I have already explained why we do not think that this type of notification would be appropriate for local, short-term collections. It also rather pre-judges the outcome of the proposed further consultation in relation to notification requirements.

The noble Lord said: Amendment No. 183C relates to Clause 47 and the exemption for local short-term collections. The Government have very kindly tabled Amendment No. 183NAB, which I think is designed to answer some of the points that I seek to raise.

Clause 47 details how public charitable collections can be exempt from the requirement to obtain a public collections certificate. One of the requirements to obtain exempt status, as specified under subsection (1)(a), is that the appeal is "local in character". It has been brought to our attention by the Institute of Fundraising that there is no definition of what constitutes a "local, short-term collection". 4.15 p.m.

The exemption for local, short-term collections will require promoters only to notify the local authority of their intention to collect; a permit or public charitable collection certificate will not be required. As I have said, the meaning of "local, short-term collection" is not defined, except in a very circular way, in the Bill. Under Clause 60(1)(d) regulations will be made setting out what constitutes a local, short-term collection.

The Institute of Fundraising believes that any definition should ensure, first, that smaller local charities fall under the exemption; and, secondly, that all charities are required to fulfil regulations which are proportionate to their resources and to the scale of their overall collection.

The matter is further complicated as the definitions may relate to the size and nature of the collection and not to the size of the charity promoting the collection. Therefore some major national charities or branches of charities might fall under the exemption if they were
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC15
 
to conduct a local small collection even though they would have the resources to fulfil the administration required for promoters of larger collections.

This is the crux of the reasoning behind the amendment. Exemptions on local grounds should be available only to small charities which do not have the staff or resources to carry out the administration required for larger appeals. Inserting the wording suggested in the amendment in the Bill would achieve this. It would also clarify the definitions that need to be given and not leave this important distinction dependant on regulations which have yet to be made.

Clause 47 is important. It provides for exempt local, short-term collections that will not need to meet the full requirements of the licensing regime. The exemption is there to ensure that we do not stifle local fundraising activity by burdening it with a requirement for licences. It is important that we achieve the right balance with these proposals so that small-scale, locally organised collections can operate without the need for licences while ensuring that the level is such as to prevent the risk of bogus fundraising activity falling, if you like, beneath the regulatory radar.

21 Mar 2005 : Column GC16
 
These include reference to the extent of the area within which the appeal is to be conducted; whether the appeal forms part of a series of appeals; and the number of collectors making the appeal and whether or not they are doing so for remuneration. We intend, as ever, to consult widely with the sector, local authorities and other interested bodies over the regulations which would set the precise boundaries of what could be considered a local short-term collection to ensure that the exemption will be set at the right level. Prescribing the precise terms of the exemption regulations would also provide flexibility in future when we needed to update them, should that prove necessary.

Clause 50 [Grounds for refusing to issue a certificate]:

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts moved Amendment No. 183D:

    Page 51, line 17, after second "the" insert "overall"

The noble Lord said: The amendment relates to Clause 50, which in turn relates to the grounds on which the commission may refuse an application for a public collections certificate. One of the grounds, under subsection (1)(e), is that,

    "it appears to the Commission that the amount likely to be applied for charitable, benevolent or philanthropic purposes in consequence of the proposed collections would be inadequate, having regard to the likely amount of the proceeds of the collections".

Our amendment would insert the word "overall" before the word "amount". We argue that the issue of returns on fundraising activities is more complex than the consideration of a simple amount, as implied by the current drafting of the Bill. The collections of direct debits on the street, for instance, produce a favourable return on the investment, but the initial cost of recruiting an individual in that manner is high. Face-to-face collection has been particularly attractive to younger donors, allowing charities to recruit new donors with a different demographic profile.

Running a collection is about more than simply bringing in new money. Other forms of fundraising, such as legacy fundraising, have much lower costs, but the visibility and marketing value to a charity of being present on the street on a busy Saturday is invaluable.

Collecting in that manner also allows fundraisers to establish a dialogue with a potential donor and begin a relationship that can be built on.

We appreciate that the commission should refuse to issue public collections certificates if it suspects that the overall amount to be applied for charitable purposes, allowing for the long-term nature of some aspects of some aspects of collecting activity, will be inadequate, but that should be reflected in the wording in the Bill. I beg to move.

Lord Bassam of Brighton: Clause 50 provides the grounds on which the Charity Commission may rely in refusing an application for a public collections certificate. A promoter planning to undertake public charitable collections, other than exempt local short-term collection, will need to apply for a public collections certificate from the Charity Commission. The Bill gives the Charity Commission the function of determining and issuing public collections certificates to organisations that it is satisfied are eligible to conduct collections. The Commission will consult widely and publish guidance on how it proposes to undertake its role in the public collections scheme. The public collections certificate could be in force for a period of up to five years.

The grounds for refusal of a public collections certificate are listed in subsection (1), and include, for example, that the applicant has been convicted of a relevant offence, or when the applicant has failed to exercise due diligence in carrying out previous collections. It is not my intention to explain each ground, as the list of grounds is necessarily long. The grounds have to cover all the possible circumstances that could arise in which the commission would be expected—in the public interest, as much as anything else—to refuse to issue a public collections certificate.

This amendment would alter the ground in subsection (e). As the provision is currently drafted, it would enable the commission to refuse to issue a public collections certificate where it appeared to the commission that the amount likely to be applied for charitable, benevolent or philanthropic purposes in consequence of the proposed collections would be inadequate, having regard to the likely amount of the proceeds of the collections. The amendment would refer to the "overall" amount. The noble Lord explained his amendment clearly, and I certainly understand the point about visibility; in marketing, that is an important concept. However, the amendment would not improve the commission's ability to apply sophisticated and reasonable judgment when assessing what return on collections would be regarded as inadequate.

We rest on the argument that the commission is in a better position than local authorities to make an informed and reasonable assessment, in full knowledge
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC19
 
of the wider fundraising environment, and not restricting its consideration to returns on the basis of any single collection, but to the returns over a series of collections. It is an important ground for refusal, to ensure that the commission would not be obliged to issue a certificate to an organisation or person it had investigated and found at fault in this regard. I do not believe that it is a ground that the commission would need routinely to rely on, but it needs to be there for the rare cases when it would be required. In these rare cases, it would be necessary because of the need to ensure the protection of the public, which we see as a very important component of the licensing scheme.

The noble Lord's point about visibility and not being judged simply on what can be collected at one place at one time is relevant, but we believe that we can deal with that issue in the guidance that is to be published. Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts moved Amendment No. 183E:

The noble Lord said: This moves us fractionally down page 51 of the Bill, but we are still on Clause 50 and the grounds for refusing to issue a certificate. Paragraph (1)(f) stipulates that the certificate may be refused,

    "if it appears to the Commission that the applicant or any other person would be likely to receive an excessive amount by way of remuneration in connection with the collections".

The amendment seeks to change the wording of the Bill, so that "excessive amount" is replaced by "inappropriate amount".

Charities use a mix of paid employees, volunteers and subcontractors to perform fundraising activities. There is a variety of employment and volunteering opportunities within the sector and, indeed, within organisations within the sector. Remuneration of paid roles increasingly reflects the level of skill required by the individual involved. Where individuals are expected to bring in millions of pounds in income—and with the responsibility of directing fundraising activities for the larger organisations, such as the NSPCC or Cancer Research UK, including overseeing public collections and other activities—the salary levels will reflect that responsibility and may well be of the order of £50,000 per annum or higher. Employed fundraisers with less experience and skills will be remunerated accordingly.

Charities may also use volunteers in some capacity, perhaps to run a local event or to collect money at a supermarket. Within smaller organisations, such as local community groups, there may be no paid employees at all.

Charities might also use a fundraising consultant or professional fundraising organisation. Payment will reflect the consultant's experience or the level of service that the professional fundraising organisation is to deliver. Such arrangements will be governed by contract. While it is important that the Charity Commission may refuse a public collections certificate if it believes that excessive payment to fundraisers may occur, it needs to have a clear understanding of the complex issue of the remuneration of fundraisers.
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC21

Lord Bassam of Brighton: The reason that there is a provision for this power to request information in respect of the commission's functions under this chapter is that the public charitable collections provisions apply to philanthropic and benevolent organisations and purposes, as well as to charities and charitable purposes. The commission already has powers under Section 9 of the Charities Act 1993 to "require" information relating to any charity, a point picked up by the noble Lord, Lord Phillips. The powers in Section 9 do not extend to philanthropic and benevolent organisations—hence the need for the provision in the Bill relating to the commission's role in licensing public collections.

The amendment raises two points: first, whether the commission should give reasons when requesting information under this section; and, secondly, whether there should be a right of appeal against a commission request for information made under the power. I shall deal with each point in turn.

There is no objection in principle to giving reasons for requests for information in most cases, but there may be circumstances in which it would harm an investigation. If, for example, the commission was investigating allegations of fraud, it might want to request information or documents to determine whether there was cause for concern and further action. Giving prior notice of the reasons for the request for information may not be in the public interest and could provide an opportunity for evidence to be destroyed before the commission could use its protective powers or refer the matter to the police.

We do not believe that a right of appeal is necessary for this power for the commission to "request" information relevant to the exercise of its functions in relation to public charitable collections. It is very different from the commission's power in Section 9 of the 1993 Act which enables it to "require" information relating to charities, and which gives rise to a right of appeal to the new tribunal. Where the commission requests information under this section, the person to whom the request was made could simply refuse.

The commission would have no right to demand the information, and the only consequence of someone refusing to provide the requested information to the commission might be that the commission decides to refuse to award a public collections certificate, or to withdraw or vary an existing public collections certificate—all of which give rise to appeal rights to the tribunal.     "This clause enables the Charity Commission to direct that a public collections certificate in force be transferred from its holder(s) to another trustee(s) within the same unincorporated charity, where the holder(s) make such a request, and all the trustees of the charity consent to the transfer".

Clause 52(3)(a) to (c) explains that the commission may direct that the certificate be transferred only when it is satisfied that each of the holders is a trustee of an unincorporated charity; that each of the recipients is a trustee of that charity; and that every trustee of the charity consents to the transfer.

We feel that this is unduly restrictive. It is unnecessary for the commission to be given the power to interfere to such a degree. Is it entirely necessary for the commission to scrutinise a certificate transfer to such an extent that the consent of every trustee of the charity is assured?

I would argue that the wording in Amendment No. 183EB is sufficient. The revised requirement that the governing body of a charity, acting as a body, can make the request to the commission when a certificate is to be transferred would ensure that the transfer is appropriately regulated while avoiding overly intrusive regulation from the commission. These tasks could be particularly onerous for the commission as there could be a large number of transfers.
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC27
 

Amendment No. 183EB strikes the balance of regulation at a more appropriate level and will ensure a less restrictive transfer of certificates than presently provided for in the Bill. I beg to move.

The Deputy Chairman of Committees (Baroness Fookes): If the amendment is agreed to, I cannot call Amendments Nos. 183F or 183G, by reason of pre-emption.

Lord Phillips of Sudbury: I support the amendment. It seems practical and sensible. I also wonder whether the failure to refer in subsection (3) to "benevolent or philanthropic" organisations is an unintended omission or is otherwise accounted for.

Lord Bassam of Brighton: The amendment gives me the opportunity to explain some of the thinking behind Clause 52.

In setting up the regime for the regulation of public collections, we are hoping to achieve a system of regulation that, for the most part, will be light touch, but will give the Charity Commission the ability to consider matters in greater detail where the need arises. In that spirit, the purpose of the clause is to provide a method of transferring a public collections certificate that is easier for those involved than making individuals apply for a brand new certificate.

We have limited this provision to unincorporated charities because they are bodies with which the Charity Commission will already have existing relationships and will, therefore, know much more about them. Charities are also fully within the commission's jurisdiction in a way that philanthropic and benevolent organisations are not. This provision is not required for charitable companies, as a charitable company is a legal person and so a certificate awarded to a charitable company would not need to be transferred just because its directors changed.

In considering an application for a transfer of a certificate where members of the governing body have changed at an unincorporated, philanthropic or benevolent organisation, the commission would no doubt wish to ask for more information to make sure that it could be satisfied that the collection or collections would be properly carried out. It would also want to be satisfied that the relevant person was suitable. In practice, therefore, the commission would want to look at a request from a philanthropic or benevolent organisation to transfer a certificate in much the same way as it would look at a new application.

We do not believe, therefore, that the amendment should be taken forward, as philanthropic and benevolent organisations do not fit with the underlying purpose of this clause, which is to enable the commission to offer an expedited procedure for the transfer of a certificate for organisations that it already has a relationship with. We believe that a public collections certificate should be transferred to trustees only and not to any individual named on an application without any restrictions on who that individual might be.
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC28
 

In practice, a public collections certificate for an unincorporated organisation would be awarded to the individual or individuals responsible for that organisation. In the case of a charity, that would be the trustees. It would not be right for responsibility for collections undertaken in the name of an organisation to be transferred to, and therefore delegated to, any individual other than to another person who is responsible for the relevant charity or philanthropic or benevolent organisation.

I hope that, having heard that, the noble Lord will feel able to withdraw the amendment.

We have previously explained the two-stage nature of the process for collection certificates and permits to collect. I think that the Committee understands that. The reason that a permit is required from a local authority for collections in a public place concerns issues of capacity. Those do not arise so much in respect of door-to-door collections. I shall explain capacity issues in a bit more detail when we debate amendments to Clause 57. Clause 56 provides for the determination of applications and issue of permits by local authorities.

Subsection (3) enables the attachment of conditions to the permit. We intend here that the types of conditions will reflect local circumstances of the collection—for example, to place a limit on the number of collectors or provide that collectors must not stand in particular places, such as close to a cash point or pedestrian crossing, one on the grounds of harassment, the other on the grounds of safety.

Subsection (5) provides that the terms of any conditions attached by the local authority under subsection (3) must be consistent with the provisions of regulations made under Clause 60, which enables regulations prescribing the types of conditions that can be applied to public collections certificates or permits. That would prevent the local authority attaching
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC31
 
conditions inconsistent with regulations providing for the conduct of public collections issued under Clause 60. Therefore, local authorities could not attach arbitrary or unfair conditions to permits. There is also the right of appeal against a local authority's decision not to issue a permit or to attach conditions to it, provided in Clause 59.

So the amendment is unnecessary because the measure is not as draconian or extensive as it has been perceived. It will simply enable local authorities to attach sensible conditions to permits, not conditions that will in any way frustrate the purpose of the collection. That is not what we want; we would not want local authorities to do that.

Clause 57 [Refusal of permits]:

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts moved Amendment No. 183HA:

    Page 57, line 30, leave out from "area" to end of line 31.

The noble Lord said: This amendment brings us to the knotty issues of Clause 57 and the refusal of permits. The clause details the grounds on which a local authority may refuse an application for a permit to conduct a collection in a public place. Subsection (1)(b) states that a refusal may be given if the proposed collection is to be,

    "conducted on a day on which another collection in a public place is already authorised under this Chapter to be conducted in the authority's area, or on the day falling immediately before, or immediately after, any such day".

Our amendment would remove the last phrase,

    "or on the day falling immediately before, or immediately after, any such day".

The reasoning behind it is quite simple. A local authority unfavourably disposed to local collections could use the power in the Bill to restrict local collections to only two days per week and, indeed, to avoid any collections at all on Saturdays.

Consider the following example: a local authority gives permission for a collection on a Tuesday. That means under the Bill as drafted that no collection could take place on Monday or Wednesday. The local
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC32
 
authority then permits a further collection on the Friday, thus ruling out Thursday and, most importantly, Saturday. The net effect is to have only two collections per week. Surely the real issue is the nature of the locality in which collections take place—the size, footfall, and so on—linked to the day of the week on which the collection is to take place. The frequency of collections in Birmingham or central Manchester should be quite different from that in my local town of Ludlow—or, dare I say it, Sudbury. So we argue that there should be no artificial, statutory barriers of the type envisaged on page 57, in lines 30 and 31. A collection every day in a locality with a big catchment area and a large passing population could be perfectly satisfactory, whereas one collection a month might be enough in a small market town.

I have no wish to attack the Minister before he has spoken, but in this group there are government Amendments Nos. 183HB and 183HE. We feel that those amendments make the matter worse. The Government have failed to remove that very difficult last phrase, which would permit a local authority to restrict collections to two days per week. In addition, Amendment No. 183HB inserts additional words on page 57, in line 31, and in particular proposed new paragraph (b)(ii), which states,

    "it appears to the authority that as a result . . . the collection . . . would itself be unlikely to be successful".

Who is going to judge what successful means? A local authority—judging that?

We have discussed the problems of short term and long term. Having a relatively unsuccessful financial short term might give a greater effect long term. A charity might do something to raise its profile in a locality or in the country as a whole. A local authority has no expertise on which to judge whether a collection will or will not be likely to be successful. I should have thought that the charity should decide whether it wishes to deploy its effort or resources and live with the consequences of whether it is successful.

You can think of situations in which a local authority might permit one set of charities to collect and not another, for all sorts of reasons not connected with the cause itself. Today I was faxed an example, which stated that,

    "the Town Centre Manager of Liverpool City Council has told the PFRA that he does not believe that the people of Liverpool will give by direct debit and has therefore set a limit of 6 fundraisers in the whole of Liverpool City Centre. This is unfortunately not an isolated incident, and I have had many local authority officers tell me that face-to-face fundraising 'will not work here' when the charities' experience is exactly the opposite".

I look forward to hearing from the Minister the thinking behind the Government's amendments, but I have very grave doubts about the original drafting, and my doubts have not been assuaged by the new amendments or by the public reaction that I have received since they were tabled. I beg to move.

On Question, Whether Clause 64 shall stand part of the Bill?
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC45
 

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: Clause 64 amends Section 60 of the 1992 Act and relates to the statements indicating benefits for charitable institutions and fundraisers, but it will be ineffective and will repeat past mistakes. Prior to the Charities Act 1992, the charity sector became aware that some unscrupulous companies and fundraisers were implying that their promotions and/or collections were being carried out in the name of charity when in fact only tiny sums were being passed to charity. By requiring those companies/professional fundraising organisations—PFOs—to state the basis on which they were being paid, it was hoped to outlaw that practice.

What happened in practice, however, was that the provisions of the 1992 Act have not worked as planned. They have created a number of problems. Importantly, the required statements of the PFOs were so vague as to be unhelpful. The wording of the Act allowed companies or PFOs to make statements which were within the letter of the law, setting out in general terms the basis of their remuneration, but which were not particularly helpful or meaningful to potential donors. These statements have created an uneven and extremely misleading fundraising playing field. Statements are not required when the promotion or collection is undertaken by a charity or a charity's trading company. That distorts the picture on fundraising, particularly when charities outsource some or all of their fundraising programmes. It is clearly inconsistent.

Two examples demonstrate this. A collection undertaken by paid staff of a charity would not require a statement to be made but the real cost-benefit ratio after taking into account the overhead costs of the staff may be the same or worse than a collection undertaken by a PFO on an out-sourced basis. Similarly, with direct mail donation campaigns, generally no statement is required as the mailing is done in the name of the charity. Donors may mistakenly think, therefore, that no paid fundraisers are involved, and that long-suffering volunteers stuffed the envelopes in the old-fashioned way. In fact, today most major direct mail campaigns involve paid consultants at a not inconsiderable cost.

Clearly, statements do not help the public to understand charity fundraising; if anything, they perpetuate public misunderstanding. In particular, the statements do not take account of other non-monetary and related benefits. In the experience of the Charity Law Association, charity fundraising is rarely undertaken only to raise money. As we have discussed this afternoon, many fundraising methods have the related aims of raising the profile of the charity, increasing the names on its database for future fundraising, for initiating campaigning and for other purposes. The current statements give donors, at best, only one side of the story and do not enable them to put the cost/benefit of the fundraising exercise in any kind of context.

The statements also fail to take account of the duties of charity trustees. In the case of fundraising initiated by charities, they fail to highlight the protection that the existing law already offers. At the end of the day,
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC46
 
it is the responsibility of charity trustees to act prudently and to evaluate the costs and benefits associated with a fundraising or awareness campaign. If they fail in this duty, they can be held accountable. The disclosure/statement regime does not help the public to understand that, but the public might have a greater degree of confidence if it did. If anything, the current regime does much to perpetuate the view that the best charities are those that spend the absolute minimum on necessary services such as administration and fundraising—which, incidentally, is contrary to the advice and guidance issued by the Charity Commission.

The statements also fail to detail the complexity of modern fundraising. Over the decades, charities have had to increase and evolve the ways in which they raise funds. Sponsored walks and flag days have increasingly been supplemented by more professional and in many cases subcontracted methods of fundraising, such as direct mail, challenge events and face-to-face fundraising on the street, house-to-house and, in the case of payroll giving, in the workplace. Similarly, commercial promotions involving a donation to a charity may involve many parties, such as wholesalers, retailers and agents. Much professional time and charity money is currently devoted to determining who and what statements need to be made in those situations.

Street collections highlight each of these points. They are generally undertaken by individuals employed and paid by PFOs. However, they and their employer are almost never the only paid professionals/PFOs involved in the campaign. The tabard-wearing fundraiser must make the required statements under the 1992 Act, and this may lead potential donors to think, first, that this is a costly method of fundraising; and, secondly, that these are the only costs associated with the fundraising. Neither is necessarily true.

The PFO may subcontract all the associated data-processing to a specialist firm to enter, edit and scan, sorting out "completed" from "failed" forms. Another specialist subcontractor may then be employed to send "thank you" letters to those who have made a donation or subscription. The chain of specialist subcontractors may extend still further, with another firm processing and establishing the consequent direct debit or standing orders. None of this is apparent to the donor at the time the gift is made.

We are not seeking to argue that street collections are not a good thing. Charities regard them as being extremely cost effective and as producing many other side benefits. But it is not clear to me how the provisions in Clause 64 deal with the problems that I have identified. If anything, they compound the problems by requiring the front-end statements to be still more specific. Accordingly, one can argue that these provisions will be even less helpful than those in the 1992 Act which they are designed to replace.

In our view, it is not possible to address the problems I have identified simply by adjusting the wording of Clause 64. It is necessary to rework
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC47
 
Sections 58 to 60 of the 1992 Act, and the amendment to the 1992 Act proposed in Clause 64 does not answer the changes that are now required.

Lord Best: The Joint Committee considered the requirement for statements indicating benefits for charitable institutions and fundraisers but came at it from a rather different perspective than that of the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts. We were worried about the new phenomenon of "chuggers"—charity muggers. These are the people who approach you not only for an instant donation on the street corner but to sign up to a standing order or direct debit which may last for many years. The Joint Committee was concerned that people should be clear of the basis on which those soliciting donations in this way were receiving remuneration, if they were. They might, of course, be volunteers, but that would be quite unusual.

The Joint Committee was well satisfied that current practices are not, as was feared, intrusive or intimidating to the people from whom the donations were sought through standing orders and direct debits. It was also impressed that this reached a market of young people—the 18 to 30 year-olds—who escape almost all other forms of fundraising. The direct approach on the street can get to such people and sign them up to a commitment which not only means that they pay their standing order or direct debit for a year or two but which can make them lifetime supporters of a particular charity.

This form of fundraising is becoming increasingly significant. The Joint Committee's concern was not that it should be prohibited—it clearly has a great deal of merit—but that the basis on which these people were making their approaches to the public on the streets should be known to the people making the donations. For example, it was the practice of one charity to pay the collector, the fundraiser, the full year's income from the standing order or direct debit for the first year of donations.

It sounds an enormous amount and disproportionate in the longer term, but it might well be justified on the grounds that people will pay for the next 10, 15 or 20 years; they will receive mailings from the charity and the amounts received will go up and up. So a whole year's donations as commission for the fundraiser might not be unreasonable in the circumstances. But those who are solicited on the streets deserve to know the basis on which the person asking for a contribution is doing so.

The Joint Committee considered that the best option was for the Bill to include a provision whereby both an identity card of the fundraiser and a written statement of the basis upon which that fundraiser was seeking ongoing donations through direct debits or standing orders from those they were approaching were required. The Government rejected this but accepted in principle that such measures were important. They hoped that self-regulation would be the mechanism through which charities would adopt good practices such as the identity card and written
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC48
 
statement, whether that is on a card or on the piece of paper when you sign up for your direct debit or standing order.

Lord Bassam of Brighton: We believe that self-regulation of fundraising should be the first resort and that the reserve power in Clause 65 is just that—a reserve power only to be used should self-regulation fail. Having heard much of the debate today, that is probably where most Members of the Committee are coming from on the Bill.

I know that there has been a lot of interest in the criteria which would be used by the Secretary of State to determine whether self-regulation of fundraising was a success. We did not consider it appropriate to publish the criteria in the Bill, given that the detail of the self-regulation scheme was not available at the time and that we would want to consult on the criteria.

Since then, the steering committee that has been developing the detail of the self-regulatory regime has published its proposals of how the scheme should work, which are available from the Institute of Fundraising. We have also published for consultation a paper on the criteria that might be used to determine the success of self-regulation, which is available on the charity law section of the Home Office website. I understand that copies have been placed in the Library.

Self-regulation will be a voluntary scheme which will promote good practice in fundraising, raise awareness and deal with complaints and the non-compliance of participants. Self-regulation would sit alongside and complement existing statutory regulations, such as the provisions on public collections that we have discussed today. It would not be expected to directly tackle fraudulent or bogus fundraising, which would remain within the remit of existing regulators, such as the local authorities, the commission and, of course, the police.
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC51
 

The noble Lord's amendment could have some undesirable consequences. For example, it would be difficult to agree on what,

    "widespread evidence of persistent failures in charity fundraising",

would mean. One interpretation is that that could make it very difficult for the Secretary of State to use that power until such time as considerable damage had been done to public confidence in fundraising.

While maintaining high levels of public confidence in the voluntary fundraising sector is the ultimate aim of the scheme, we do not propose to measure the success or failure of the scheme on the basis of changes in public confidence, as such changes may be outside the control of the self-regulatory scheme. It would be possible for there to be a particular scandal which might severely damage public confidence in charity fundraising, but which the self-regulation scheme would be able to do little to prevent.

The criteria for assessing the self-regulation scheme outlined in the consultation paper do not focus on attempts to measure public confidence. Instead, they focus on what the Government believe an effective self-regulation scheme should cover. I do not propose to list all the expectations, but I will give some examples. The scheme should promote openness, transparency and accountability in fundraising practice. The scheme will need to attract high levels of voluntary participation across the sector—although, of course, it is appreciated that it would take time to build up the numbers of participants. We also think that participants should reflect the diversity of the sector. The scheme and its members must also provide the public with a clear guarantee of what they can expect from fundraisers who are members of the scheme and from the scheme itself. There must be effective and meaningful sanctions for non-compliance that are proportionate to the nature and extent of any non-compliance.

We appreciate that it will take time for self-regulation to prove itself and we want to give it a fair wind. The consultation paper suggests that it is reviewed when we come to review the impact of the legislation—within five years of enactment. That should provide it with sufficient time to demonstrate its effectiveness.

Self-regulation remains the Government's preferred solution. We intend to publish the criteria for assessing its success following consultation. We will certainly take heed of the points raised by Members of the Committee today. On that basis, I trust that the noble Lord will feel able to withdraw his amendment.

"DATA PROTECTION AND DISABILITY CHARITIES

Lord Bassam of Brighton: The purpose of the amendment—to facilitate sharing information between public bodies and disability charities—is laudable, not least because the information gathered will be of benefit to those who use the services of charities which work in this field and do an extremely good job. But there are problems with it. The Joint Committee received a memo from the charity U Can Do I.T., whose concerns I understand. It argued, very ably, about the perception that exists about the Data Protection Act and the way in which it works to prevent a useful transfer of information between government departments and public bodies.

There is, however, no general prohibition against data sharing in the Act. It rather sets the standards which such sharing should attain. The first data protection principle states that personal data shall be processed "fairly and lawfully". They must also be processed in accordance with the various conditions set out in the legislation. There are more conditions for sensitive personal data than for data which are not sensitive. If an organisation has trouble complying with the conditions for processing sensitive personal data, it can apply to the Department for Constitutional Affairs for an order. That is the Schedule 3 order mentioned in the amendment. However, in this schedule there is already a basis for organisations to disclose data they hold, if the people affected have given their explicit consent. A Schedule 3 order can be made only if there is an important public interest at stake. There would also need to be specific and suitable safeguards to protect the fundamental rights and privacy of the individual in this case.

It is perhaps worth mentioning that if this amendment definitely gave a power to process information, as it looks as if the first paragraph does, then a Schedule 3 order would not be necessary according to provisions in the Data Protection Act. It seems to us that the fundamental problem is that the amendment would put the privacy of disabled individuals at some risk and we do not accept that that can be right. We agree with the noble Lord, Lord Swinfen, in his interpretation. There might also be some ECHR implications.

A pertinent issue here is the extent to which the potential beneficiary has given their consent to their data being shared. Under the Data Protection Act, bodies which hold lists of disabled people must ensure, among other things and so far as is practicable, that the individuals on that list are provided with a description of the purposes for which the data is to be used and any disclosures of personal data they intend to make. When the disclosure involves sensitive personal data, which includes information relating to an individual's physical or mental health or condition, then a disclosure should occur only when one of a number of specific conditions is met.
 
21 Mar 2005 : Column GC58
 

Currently, the most relevant condition is when the individual has given his explicit consent to that disclosure. Although it is not clear whether the disclosure would consist of information about an individual's particular disability, if their name was passed to a charity for the blind, for example, assumptions would arguably be made about condition of their eyesight. Provided that the organisation holding the list of disabled people make it clear that they would like to pass information about the people on their list to a particular charity and provided that they obtain the individual's consent for this disclosure, the Data Protection Act will not prevent it and the charities such as U Can Do I.T. would receive the referrals that they seek.

In addition, when someone is known to be disabled, there is nothing to prevent the body holding the list asking whether they would like some information about relevant charities. That would obviously give them the opportunity to make contact with the relevant charities themselves. However, this amendment extends to arguing that those bodies with lists of disabled people should be allowed to make routine disclosures of information about disabled individuals, regardless of whether that individual has been asked if he would like this disclosure to occur and regardless of any wish he might have to keep information about his disability private and restricted only to those who need to know it.

Although we appreciate the good intention of the proposal, we do not believe that it is the case that all charities have a legitimate need to know about the disabilities of everyone who comes into contact with a particular government department, particularly in the case of those individuals who have no interest in the work of that charity, whether they might benefit from it or not. Given that the legislation does not prohibit the sharing of data, in our view it is difficult to justify such an exemption from the fair processing requirements. The fact that somebody is disabled surely cannot mean they should have the choice to determine who processes data relating to their disability withdrawn.

The amendment does not work as the noble Lord would seek for it to work. In fact, it probably goes much further than that and could lead to some unintended and harmful consequences. If we accepted the amendment, it would certainly put at risk some people who have a particular disability and who want their privacy and individual circumstances protected and disguised. For those reasons, we could not possibly accept the amendment.

Lord Phillips of Sudbury: I am grateful for that careful and considerate reply, which I shall study carefully, as will others outside this House. Be that as it may, I beg leave to withdraw the amendment.
